        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835"><meta property="og:title" content="Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/acsomega.9b03835.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Iron-based polyanionic materials can be exploited to realize low cost, durable, and safe cathodes for both bulk and thin film sodium-ion batteries. Herein, we report pulsed laser deposited mixed phosphate Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 as a positive electrode for thin film sodium-ion microbatteries. The bulk material and thin films of Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 are employed by solution combustion synthesis (SCS) and the pulsed laser deposition (PLD) technique, respectively. Phase purity and the nature of the crystallinity of the thin films were confirmed by grazing incidence X-ray diffraction and transmission electron microscopy. Identification of surface roughness and morphology was obtained from atomic force microscopy and scanning electron microscopy, respectively. Emerging electrochemical properties were observed from charge–discharge profiles of the thin films, which are well comparable to bulk material properties. The Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 thin film electrodes delivered a highly reversible Na+ storage capacity of ∼120 mAh g–1 with an excellent stability of over 500 cycles. Electrochemical analysis results revealed that the thickness of the film affects the storage capacity."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/acsomega.9b03835.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318ade04c4788d3-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318ade04c4788d3-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'e83718e9-6b0a-4376-b1bc-80cdddb95eca'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318ade04c4788d3-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'omega';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/omega/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/omega/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

.articleHeaderDropzone5 .advertisement { display: none; }
.article_header .advertisement {
  margin: -25px auto 25px auto;
}
.show-recommended .sponsoredContent { display: none; }
.show-recommended__wrapper .sponsoredContent { display: none; }

<script nonce="9318ade04c4788d3-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "acsodf";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;Fe&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(PO&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;)&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;P&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;7&lt;/sub&gt; Thin Films for Sodium-Ion Microbatteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsomega.9b03835", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/medium/ao9b03835_0001.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.9b03835&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Iron-based polyanionic materials can be exploited to realize low cost, durable, and safe cathodes for both bulk and thin film sodium-ion batteries. Herein, we report pulsed laser deposited mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> as a positive electrode for thin film sodium-ion microbatteries. The bulk material and thin films of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> are employed by solution combustion synthesis (SCS) and the pulsed laser deposition (PLD) technique, respectively. Phase purity and the nature of the crystallinity of the thin films were confirmed by grazing incidence X-ray diffraction and transmission electron microscopy. Identification of surface roughness and morphology was obtained from atomic force microscopy and scanning electron microscopy, respectively. Emerging electrochemical properties were observed from charge–discharge profiles of the thin films, which are well comparable to bulk material properties. The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film electrodes delivered a highly reversible Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity of ∼120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with an excellent stability of over 500 cycles. Electrochemical analysis results revealed that the thickness of the film affects the storage capacity.</p></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290672&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Phosphates"> Phosphates </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291373&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Pulsed laser deposition"> Pulsed laser deposition </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290945&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Thin films"> Thin films </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have realized unprecedented commercial success in myriads of portable electronic devices such as mobile phones, laptops, and digital generators. Recently, the usage of LIBs has entered into large-scale applications such as (hybrid) electric vehicles and power grid applications. However, high cost and limited resources of lithium have raised concerns vis-à-vis the developments in large-scale applications. At this juncture, it has triggered an effort to develop alternative energy storage technology over LIBs employing earth-abundant economic elements. In this emerging era of post-Li-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have garnered much attention due to the abundance, ease of accessibility, low cost of sodium-based resources, and strikingly similar charge–discharge characteristics compared to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> It has triggered extensive research on sodium-ion batteries as an attractive alternative to LIBs, leading to numerous reports on sodium-based materials acting as cathodes <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> and anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> The ongoing quest is to design suitable electrodes with enhanced performance factors like energy density, power density, and cycling stability that are crucial for large-scale energy storage systems. In this pursuit, polyanionic-type insertion materials form a rich treasure house because of their robust three-dimensional crystal framework and tunable operating voltage. Over the years, a variety of polyanionic cathodes have been unraveled successfully. Some such candidates are the Prussian blue family, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> phosphates, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14−16)</a> fluorophosphates, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> and pyrophosphates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> Going a step further, mixed phosphate compounds form a niche class of polyanionic cathodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> One such candidate is the mixed phosphates family with the general chemical formula of Na<sub>4</sub>M<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (M = Co, Ni, Mn, Mg, or Fe), as first reported in 2001 by Sanz et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24−26)</a> These mixed phosphates have an open framework with long-range interconnected channels (M<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>) along the <i>b</i> direction, favoring fast Na<sup>+</sup>-ion migration and enhanced electrochemical performance. These long-range channels render appropriate pathways for Na<sup>+</sup> migration with a low activation energy barrier. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Moreover, it has been reported that there are four crystallographically distinct Na<sup>+</sup> sites: even if some channels are blocked by defects, other channels are still accessible to facilitate Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction into the electrode material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> In this mixed phosphate family, the iron-based Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> analogue forms the most attractive positive electrode material for NIBs owing to the low cost/toxicity, an abundance of iron-based resources, and suitable energy/power density. Besides, it can be easily prepared by conventional solid-state synthesis and works as a 3.1 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>) sodium battery cathode involving multiple-electron redox reaction delivering high energy density with robust cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref27 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref27 ref29" aria-label="reference 25, 27 and 29">(25,27,29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">On another note, in addition to bulk batteries, the development of thin film microbatteries has emerged as a key sector in this era of nanotechnology. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> These microbatteries have niche applications such as flexible/wearable devices, smart cards, remote sensors, metal oxide semiconductor (CMOS) memory chips, implantable medical devices, and microelectromechanical systems (MEMS). An all-solid-state thin film microbattery is ideal in this context. Significant efforts have been devoted to searching advanced thin film electrode materials to enhance capacity and to improve stability for all-solid-state thin film batteries. To date, very few sodium-based insertion materials have been employed in thin film batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Here, with due material optimization (like nanosizing), we have implemented mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> in thin film batteries for the first time by pulsed laser deposition. It delivers robust electrochemical performance for thin film Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of Iron-Based Mixed Phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> product was synthesized by the solution combustion synthesis (SCS) route. In a typical synthesis process, 1.19 g of NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, 3.03 g of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O (oxidizer), and 2.64 g of C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>8</sub>O<sub>6</sub> (fuel) were thoroughly dissolved in 50 mL of double-distilled water. In the initial step, the precursor mixture was heated at 120 °C (for 2 h) with steady magnetic stirring to evaporate the excess water. Then, it was directly transferred to a hot plate maintained at 300 °C. It led to the ascorbic acid (fuel)-triggered exothermic ignition of reactants, resulting in the formation of an amorphous intermediate product. Finally, this product was ground to a fine powder and was annealed at 600 °C for 12 h (under steady Ar flow) to obtain a phase-pure mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (denoted as NFPPO) product.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Pulsed Laser Deposition of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> Thin Films</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The NFPPO powder was uniaxially pressed and calcined to make a circular pellet (diameter, 13 mm; thickness, 4 mm) acting as a target. Employing the pulsed laser deposition (PLD) technique, NFPPO thin films were uniformly deposited on circular stainless steel (SS) disks acting as substrates. A krypton fluoride excimer laser with a pulse energy of 150 mJ/pulse was used to ablate the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> target. The laser spot was aligned to focus on the target with a laser fluence of 2 J/cm<sup>2</sup>. The substrate-to-target distance was adjusted to an optimized value of 4.5 cm to get efficient deposition. The thin films were deposited with a continuous flow of Ar gas, maintaining the substrates at 600 °C. The PLD processing parameters such as vacuum level, laser energy, the distance between the substrate to target, gas flow, deposition time, and substrate temperature were optimized carefully to obtain homogeneous films of controlled thickness.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structure of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> bulk powder was verified by a PANalytical X’Pert Pro diffractometer equipped with Cu Kα target (λ = 1.5404 Å). Rietveld refinement was performed using the GSAS program with the EXPGUI front end. Microstructural features of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> powder were captured by an FEI Inspect F50 scanning electron microscope. Next, (HR)TEM images and selected area diffraction patterns were collected with an FEI Tecnai T20 U-Twin TEM unit. For the electrochemical study of the NFPPO bulk sample, the cathode composite was prepared using an 80:10:10 (w/w) mixture of working electrode, carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in a minimal amount of <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) to form a thick slurry, which was coated on aluminum foil and was dried under vacuum. Initially, we studied the activity of the NFPPO system in CR2032-type coin cells by using 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate acting as the electrolyte and sodium metal foil as the counter electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure of the PLD deposited NFPPO thin films was characterized by grazing incidence X-ray diffraction (GIXRD; SmartL, Bruker) using Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5406 Å). The surface morphology and roughness were estimated by atomic force microscopy (AFM; Veeco dII system). Microstructural studies and average thickness of the film were estimated by FE-SEM (Zeiss Ultra) analysis. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) was used to confirm the crystalline phase of NFPPO thin films. The electrochemical properties of NFPPO thin films were evaluated in CR2032 coin cells, as mentioned in a previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> These cells were galvanostatically cycled at different rates in the potential range of 1.8–3.8 V using a Bio-Logic (Claix, France) BCS-810 battery cycler.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The Rietveld refined XRD pattern and corresponding structural model of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> end-member are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The resulting lattice parameters were determined to be <i>a</i> = 18.0165(16) Å, <i>b</i> = 6.5510(6) Å, <i>c</i> = 10.6972(9) Å, and unit cell volume = 1262.54(24) Å<sup>3</sup> for the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> end-member assuming an orthorhombic structure with the <i>Pn</i>2<sub>1</sub><i>a</i> space group. The structure can be described as a 3D network of corner-sharing (Fe<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>)<sub>∞</sub> infinite layers parallel to the <i>b</i>–<i>c</i> plane comprising three crystallographically independent iron atoms in octahedra abridged by PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. These layers are interconnected along the <i>a</i> axis by (P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) pyrophosphate groups (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, inset). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> is stable until 600 °C, above which it undergoes phase decomposition to form a mixture of NaFePO<sub>4</sub> (maricite) and Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Therefore, the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> mixed phosphate framework was found to form and remain stable at temperatures ranging from 400 to 600 °C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/medium/ao9b03835_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0004.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Rietveld refined XRD pattern of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> sample prepared at 600 °C. The experimental data (red stars), simulated pattern (black line), their difference (blue line), and Bragg positions (pink ticks) are shown. The goodness of fit values obtained from the refinement were Rp =1.30, Rwp = 2.28, and χ<sup>2</sup> = 1.61. The inset illustrates the crystal structure with alternate arrays consisting of PO<sub>4</sub> and P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> units. (b) GIXRD pattern of the PLD deposited Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film. The peaks with asterisk marks the stem from the stainless steel (SS) substrate.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.9b03835&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After the PLD deposition of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films on stainless steel substrates (deposited at 600 °C), the phase purity of the deposited thin film was confirmed by GIXRD, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. The observed XRD pattern of the thin film was in good agreement with the corresponding bulk powder with no evidence of any secondary phases. Three characteristic peaks for Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> in the 2θ range between 35 and 40° were obtained along with two sharp peaks at 45 and 51° arising from the SS substrate. By carefully optimizing all thin film deposition parameters such as laser energy, partial pressure (vacuum level), and deposition temperature during the PLD process, we could obtain phase-pure thin film target products.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a displays the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of bulk Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> particles prepared by the SCS route. It confirms the formation of highly agglomerated micrometric particles. The dehydration reaction involving direct condensation polymerization of phosphate groups leads to the formation of the micrometric particles. A closer look by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) revealed secondary near-spherical nanometric Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> particles with thin layers of carbon coating arising due to the decomposition of carbonaceous precursor (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c). The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> particles are uniformly coated by in situ deposited 3D interconnected networks of carbon. The uniform carbon coating enhances the electronic conductivity of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> particle network, which is beneficial during the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process. SEM elemental mapping and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) were carried out for Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> materials to confirm the constituent elements and the homogeneous distribution of all the elements in these materials given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/suppl_file/ao9b03835_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1 and Table S1</a>. The high-resolution TEM image of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c reveals the crystallinity with lattice fringes having an interplanar spacing of 0.907 nm, corresponding to the (200) lattice planes of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> mixed phosphate product.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/medium/ao9b03835_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphology of the bulk Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> powder: (a) SEM, (b) TEM, and (c) high-resolution TEM images showing lattice fringes. Thin film surface morphology: (d) SEM, (inset: cross-sectional image), (e) three-dimensional AFM, and (f) high-resolution TEM images (inset showing the SAED pattern).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.9b03835&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of PLD deposited Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films were obtained from SEM and atomic force microscopy (AFM). It confirmed the uniform deposition of nanostructured target thin films (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). The PLD-grown thin films exhibited crystalline grains with a homogeneous distribution of well-dispersed particles. The particle agglomeration is almost negligible as compared to bulk samples owing to the formation of uniform nanoparticles obtained by laser deposition. The surface morphology of thin films exhibited a smooth surface and small crystalline grains in a size range of 65–75 nm. The average thickness of the thin film was ∼220 nm, as measured from cross-sectional SEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, inset). The three-dimensional AFM image depicted uniform surface morphology with a near homogeneous distribution of well-crystallized grains with less degree of agglomeration (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e). The surface roughness of the film was found to be ∼11 nm. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f represents the high-resolution TEM image of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film, confirming the highly crystalline nature of the PLD-coated thin films. The corresponding interplanar spacing was determined to be 0.450 nm, related to the (202) lattice plane of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> mixed phosphate.</div><div class="NLM_p">First, galvanostatic (dis)charge analysis of the bulk Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> sample was performed in Na-ion half-cell architecture in the voltage range of 1.8–3.8 V. Representative voltage profiles registered at a current rate of 0.1 C are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The voltage profiles show a stable voltage profile with three plateau regions around 3.2, 2.9, and 2.5 V (during discharge). These reversible multiple plateau regions stem from (de)insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from different Na sites in the parent structure. There are four Na sites present in the crystal framework where three Na sites favor efficient Na<sup>+</sup>-ion (de)insertion, while the fourth Na site works as spectator ions enhancing the structural stability during the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion (de)insertion process.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/medium/ao9b03835_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> mixed phosphate cathode in sodium half-cell architecture. (a) Charge–discharge voltage profiles of bulk Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> powder. Charge–discharge voltage profiles of thin film electrodes of the thicknesses of (b) 220 and (c) 300 nm. (d) Corresponding differential capacity vs voltage (dQ/dV) plots for 10 cycles. (e) Comparative voltage profiles of the thin film (thickness, 220 nm) electrode cycled at different rates and (f) capacity retention at 1C rate and Couloumbic efficiency for 500 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/asset/images/large/ao9b03835_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsomega.9b03835&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A slight downshift was observed between the first and subsequent cycles due to an irreversible structural rearrangement during the first charge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The following charge–discharge cycles (10 cycles) were very stable, exhibiting a high degree of reversibility. This carbon-coated Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode delivered an initial discharge capacity of 126 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (i.e., 97% of its theoretical capacity). Though the capacity dropped to 121 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the second cycle, it was very steady in subsequent cycles. The observed discharge capacity of ∼121 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to 2.8 Na<sup>+</sup> intercalations per formula unit.</div><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by the robust performance of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode in bulk form, its electrochemical performance of thin film form was tested in a CR2032 coin-type half-cell fabricated with the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film as a cathode, sodium metal as a counter electrode, and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a mixture of ethylene carbonate and dimethyl carbonate (1:1 EC:DMC) acting as an electrolyte. Upon cycling in a potential window of 1.8–3.8 V (vs Na), the thin films with a thickness of ∼220 nm exhibited galvanostatic charge–discharge curves very similar to the bulk Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> cathode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). It was very robust with no capacity fading during the first 100 cycles. At ambient temperature, this ∼220 nm thin film delivered a capacity of ∼118 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, very similar to the capacity of the bulk sample, retaining most of the characteristic features like the occurrence of three distinct plateaus with an average working potential of ∼3 V. The nanosized grains with well-dispersed surface morphology are key to get excellent electrochemical performance in thin film batteries. The crystallinity, grain size, and surface morphology affect the final electrochemical properties of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films. Further, the open and stable polyanion crystal structure is beneficial for fast ion transport and plays a vital role in practical applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Overall, the robust electrochemical performance of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films can be useful for all-solid-state batteries. However, the electrochemical properties were affected as a function of film thickness. Thicker (∼300 nm) films led to reduced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, thereby merging three distinct plateaus to a single sloping plateau with a lower average working potential of ∼2.7 V (vs Na) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). The optimized electrochemical activity was obtained for a film thickness of ∼220 nm, in which case, three distinct oxidation–reduction peaks involving Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> oxidation–reduction centered at ∼3 V were observed in the differential capacity (dQ/dV) plot (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). They are related to the different sodium sites present in the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The rate capability of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film cathode tested at various current rates is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. Without any optimization, it provides a capacity of ∼125 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a low current rate of 2 μA cm<sup>–2</sup>. Even at faster cycling (∼10 μA cm<sup>–2</sup>), it delivered a capacity of ∼110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating excellent rate capability stemming from large Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels in this material. Cycling stability and Coulombic efficiency of the ∼220 nm thin film sample was tested up to 500 cycles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f. The enlarged region of the Coulombic efficiency plot is given in the Supporting Information (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/suppl_file/ao9b03835_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). It exhibited no significant capacity fading and a high Coulombic efficiency of ∼100%, confirming the highly reversible nature of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin film cathode. The three-dimensional Na<sup>+</sup>-ion conducting pathways along with a low activation barrier for the fast Na<sup>+</sup>-ion migration, morphology, and carbon coating reinforce the material stability, which, in turn, allows this battery to operate for a long time.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, Fe-based mixed polyanionic Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films were prepared by pulsed laser deposition due to optimization during film deposition led to crystalline Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films with uniform grain size and surface morphology (absence of agglomeration). When implemented in Na-ion half-cells, these thin films exhibited excellent electrochemical performance with robust cycling stability, similar to the bulk batteries. To the best of our knowledge, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> thin films (∼220 nm) deliver reasonable electrochemical performance with good reversibility among all sodium insertion materials. The mixed phosphate Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> forms an inexpensive and safe cathode for designing all-solid-state sodium-ion microbatteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes3"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">EDS spectrum, SEM image, elemental mapping images of the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> powder sample, EDS analysis results table, and the Coulombic efficiency plot (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/suppl_file/ao9b03835_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsomega.9b03835/suppl_file/ao9b03835_si_001.pdf">ao9b03835_si_001.pdf (310.73 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsomega.9b03835" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.  Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seo, D. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrode Materials for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries: Potential Alternatives to Current Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">710</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">721</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201200026</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D710-721%26journal%3DAdv.%2BEnergy%2BMater.%26author%3DS.%2BW.%2BKim%26author%3DD.%2BH.%2BSeo%26author%3DX.%2BMa%26author%3DG.%2BCeder%26author%3DK.%2BKang%26title%3DElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%253A%2BPotential%2BAlternatives%2Bto%2BCurrent%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Faenm.201200026&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.9b03835&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Faenm.201200026&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">710-721</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S. T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y. K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span>. <i>Chem. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>46</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C6CS00776G</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D46%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D3529-3614%26journal%3DChem.%2BSoc.%2BRev.%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BHwang%26author%3DS.%2BT.%2BMyung%26author%3DY.%2BK.%2BSun%26title%3DSodium-ion%2Bbatteries%253A%2Bpresent%2Band%2Bfuture%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC6CS00776G&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsomega.9b03835&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC6CS00776G&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03192J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),

        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">81-102</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703055"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lander, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanionic Insertion Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A layered phase, NaNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 (NaNMC), isostructural with NaCoO2, was synthesized.  Stoichiometric NaNMC crystallizes in a rhombohedral R3-m space group where Na is in an octahedral environment (O3-Type).  Galvanostatic cycling on NaNMC vs. Na cell indicated a reversible intercalation of 0.5 Na, leading to a capacity of 120 mA-h/g in the voltage range of 2-3.75 V and indicating its possible application in Na-ion batteries.  The electrochem. driven Na insertion/de-insertion in NaNMC is assocd. with several phase transitions and solid soln. regimes which are studied by in situ XRD.  Na de-insertion in NaxNMC resulted in sequential phase transitions composed of biphasic and monophasic domains.  The compn. driven structural evolution in NaxNMC follows the sequence O3 → O1 → P3 → P1 phases with an increased 'c' parameter, while the 'a' parameter remains almost unchanged.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kajiyama, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iwatate, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishikawa, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hitomi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okuyama, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Usui, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-type Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>[Fe<sub>1/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries</span>. <i>Nat.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.  However, their reversible capacity is typically limited to 100 mA-h/g.  Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.  These results will contribute to the development of rechargeable batteries from the earth-abundant elements operable at room temp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA03349C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iyo, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High stable post-spinel NaMn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> cathode of sodium ion battery</span>. <i>J.
